  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

But, as William fills his pages with reports of local club meetings and pictures of humorously shaped vegetables, dark forces high up in Ankh-Morpork's society are plotting to overthrow the city's ruler, Lord Vetinari.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/274836212" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/274836212?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer274836212" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17625488790151133405"><br/>I think I've only read this Pratchett novel once before, and on the re-read, I enjoyed it more than I expected to. <br/><br/>Don't get me wrong. The worst Terry Pratchett novel is still wonderfully enjoyable. And while I don't think this one is *the* best, it's *among* the best. Definitely on his A list. <br/><br/>As a bonus, I think this book would be more accessible to new readers, as most of the main characters are new, and the older characters are mostly there for support. <br/><br/>As I re-read all the Discworld novels,</span>
  <span id="freeText17625488790151133405" style="display:none"><br />I think I've only read this Pratchett novel once before, and on the re-read, I enjoyed it more than I expected to. <br /><br />Don't get me wrong. The worst Terry Pratchett novel is still wonderfully enjoyable. And while I don't think this one is *the* best, it's *among* the best. Definitely on his A list. <br /><br />As a bonus, I think this book would be more accessible to new readers, as most of the main characters are new, and the older characters are mostly there for support. <br /><br />As I re-read all the Discworld novels, one of the things that I appreciate more and more is their optimism. After reading one of his books I get the feeling that the world is a good place. There may be good people and bad people, but if you're one of the good folks, things will work out okay if you do your best and catch a little good luck. <br /><br />I need to feel that way sometimes. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17625488790151133405" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1924865827" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1924865827?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1924865827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10893002886638076764">The Truth Shall Make Ye Fret.<br/><br/>So it says on the cover of the Ankh-Morpork Times. Printer’s error? Maybe. Maybe Terry Pratchett is up to his old tricks and this is a monster truck rally of Have-At-You!! intended to satirize journalism, government, free speech, and whatever else might get in the way.<br/><br/>In 1980 Sting, lead singer, songwriter and bass player for The Police sang <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7v2GDbEmjGE' rel='nofollow noopener'>this:</a><br/><br/>“Poets priests and politicians<br/>Have words to thank for their positions<br/>Words that scream for your submission<br/>And no-one's jam</span>
  <span id="freeText10893002886638076764" style="display:none">The Truth Shall Make Ye Fret.<br><br>So it says on the cover of the Ankh-Morpork Times. Printer’s error? Maybe. Maybe Terry Pratchett is up to his old tricks and this is a monster truck rally of Have-At-You!! intended to satirize journalism, government, free speech, and whatever else might get in the way.<br><br>In 1980 Sting, lead singer, songwriter and bass player for The Police sang <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7v2GDbEmjGE" rel="nofollow noopener">this:</a><br><br>“Poets priests and politicians<br>Have words to thank for their positions<br>Words that scream for your submission<br>And no-one's jamming their transmission<br>'Cos when their eloquence escapes you<br>Their logic ties you up and rapes you<br><br>De do do do de da da da<br>Is all I want to say to you”<br><br>In 2000, Terry Pratchett first published his 25th Discworld novel – The Truth. It’s about words. It’s about a free press, the first of it’s kind in Ankh-Morpork, and about the social, cultural and economic forces that weigh upon us and all of our decisions. It’s also about relationships and family and internal elements of our nature that keep us moving forward.<br><br>Like most of Pratchett’s work, there are ubiquitous and entertaining references and allusions to pop culture, history, art and whatever else strikes the fancy of this extraordinary writer – my favorite here being numerous Watergate references.<br><br>Just as in Neil Gaiman’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14497.Neverwhere__London_Below___1_" title="Neverwhere (London Below, #1) by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Neverwhere</a>, two criminals make an attempt to steal the show and damn near do. Mr. Pin and Mr. Tulip are the Vincent and Jules of the Discworld: dangerous loose cannons with good lines.<br><br>Another excellent entry into the Discworld universe.<br><br>“Because nothing has to be true forever. Just for long enough, to tell you the truth.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1547646169i/26925962.jpg" width="248" height="241" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="10893002886638076764" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_888932021" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/888932021" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/888932021?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780380978953" href="/book/show/325365.The_Truth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=humorous-fiction">humorous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer888932021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer68015205158426158"><b>
  <i>"The truth shall make ye <s>free</s> fret"</i>
</b><br/><br/> Despite their fear of that newfangled movable type, Ankh-Morpork has FINALLY gotten a newspaper!<br/><br/>Young William de Worde is quite surprised when his page full of "things written down" is suddenly incredibly popular with the citizenry, and publishing a newspaper becomes a rather terrifying, learn-as-you-go experience. Should he listen to the advice of others or go with his gut?<br/><br/><b>"Be careful. People like to be told what they already know. Remember that. They get</b></span>
  <span id="freeText68015205158426158" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"The truth shall make ye <s>free</s> fret"</i>
</b><br /><br /> Despite their fear of that newfangled movable type, Ankh-Morpork has FINALLY gotten a newspaper!<br /><br />Young William de Worde is quite surprised when his page full of "things written down" is suddenly incredibly popular with the citizenry, and publishing a newspaper becomes a rather terrifying, learn-as-you-go experience. Should he listen to the advice of others or go with his gut?<br /><br /><b>"Be careful. People like to be told what they already know. Remember that. They get uncomfortable when you tell them <i>new</i> things. New things...well, new things aren't what they expect. They like to know that, say, a dog will bite a man. That is what dogs do. They don't want to know that a man bites a dog because the world is not supposed to happen like that. In short, what people <i>think</i> they want is news, but what they really crave is <i>olds</i>."</b><br /><br />One thing William discovers early on is that people LIKE seeing their names (and ages, for some reason) in the paper, and soon he is overwhelmed with potential "news" items like which was the coldest winter ever and vegetables that resemble body parts. <br /><br />Before long, he's got some competition in the form of <b>THE INQUIRER</b>, a tabloid that features headlines like <b>
  <i>Elves Stole My Husband</i>
</b>. This inexplicably popular, lying fish-wrapper may even put HIS little paper out of business.<br /><br />And then there's that matter of William and his staff "helping" the Watch solve a mystery involving Lord Vetinari...<br /><br />We meet some great new characters in this book, including Harry King, a combination night soil man/recycling czar, and Otto, a vampire who's trying <b>REALLY HARD</b> NOT to be such a bloodsucker.<br /><br />This is a terrifically funny outing. One of my favorite moments was when the search for a missing dog leads to a <s>parking garage</s> horse stable encounter with an informant named <i>Deep Bone</i>. And this priceless story from <i>The Ankh-Morpork Times</i>:<br /><br /><b>
  <i>A miscreant stole $200 worth of silver from H. Hogland and Son, Jewllrs., of Nonesuch Street yesterday p.m. Mr. Hogland, (32) who was threatened at knifepoint, told the Times: "I shall recognize the man if I should see him again because not many people have a stocking on their head."</i>
</b><br /> <br />Remember - <b>A lie can run around the world before the truth has got it's boots on.</b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="68015205158426158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16204752658599855850">3.5 stars<br/><br/>This was a fun book and a great introduction to William de Word, the creator of the newspaper of Ankh Morpork. He's a fun character and goes through excellent development. <br/><br/>Death makes a hilarious appearance and the Watch is scattered throughout. The Patrician also developed after approximately 26 books. <br/><br/>So, as always, I'd recommend this to fans of the Discworld series.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780552147682" href="/book/show/288680.The_Truth">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer672188421" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3032056541495735031">I have a favorable bias toward the story of someone stumbling into the business of printed news. This story isn't exactly analogous to my own. I decided to start a magazine when the concept of magazines already existed. William de Worde managed to invent the newspaper without even trying. Terry Pratchett does a wonderful job of showing the form evolve in fast-motion. <br/><br/>He also gives ample stage time here to two of my favorite Discworld characters: Lord Vetinari and Commander Vimes. And of course, </span>
  <span id="freeText3032056541495735031" style="display:none">I have a favorable bias toward the story of someone stumbling into the business of printed news. This story isn't exactly analogous to my own. I decided to start a magazine when the concept of magazines already existed. William de Worde managed to invent the newspaper without even trying. Terry Pratchett does a wonderful job of showing the form evolve in fast-motion. <br /><br />He also gives ample stage time here to two of my favorite Discworld characters: Lord Vetinari and Commander Vimes. And of course, as always, he manages to make every page humorous without making light of serious issues (which he grapples with on, well, pretty much every page). <br /><br />This was also terrific read-aloud fare thanks to the character of Mr. Tulip, who swears a lot. Sort of. Specifically, he says, "--ing" all the time. Pratchett makes it clear that he isn't doing the old-fashioned removal of profanity with a dash left behind to show where the bad word was; his character's dialogue is self-cleaning, which leaves a lot of the other characters mystified. It's a lot of fun to read Tulip's dialogue aloud with a solid working-class British accent and lean on that dash before pronouncing the "ing" part. Seriously. Try it. It sounds fantastic. <br /><br />In fact, I might just use this as a replacement habit to give up swearing, since my son's been begging me to. Kind of a nicotine patch for potty-mouths. <br /><br />Anyway. Pratchett is such a skilled writer that you can jump into the Discworld books anywhere and enjoy them just fine, but I think this one is even better than usual in that regard. If you haven't read him before, this novel is a great place to start. If you <i>have</i> read him and haven't read <i>The Truth</i> yet, what on earth are you --ing waiting for? </span>
  <a data-text-id="3032056541495735031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2841990144" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2841990144" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2841990144?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/25806442-the-truth">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2841990144" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8835651190838913166"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561572643i/27730278._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>The 25th <i>Discworld</i> novel is one about the industrial revolution on the Disc.<br/><br/>William de Word doesn't want to live the way his family has for generations. Instead, he lives in Ankh-Morpork and got a job. While he pays his bills by writing letters to different people from different other places he comes across a few industrious (see what I did there? ;P) dwarves who have invented a rather advanced printing press. Thus the <u>Ankh-Morpork Times</u> is born. The problem is not that paper is now used to spre</span>
  <span id="freeText8835651190838913166" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561572643i/27730278._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The 25th <i>Discworld</i> novel is one about the industrial revolution on the Disc.<br><br>William de Word doesn't want to live the way his family has for generations. Instead, he lives in Ankh-Morpork and got a job. While he pays his bills by writing letters to different people from different other places he comes across a few industrious (see what I did there? ;P) dwarves who have invented a rather advanced printing press. Thus the <u>Ankh-Morpork Times</u> is born. The problem is not that paper is now used to spread stories; the problem is that de Word believes in the truth and only reports about actual events.<br>At the same time, there is a plot against Lord Vetinari (again). And the conspirators are bringing in outside help (their first mistake).<br>Soon, there is a feud between Ankh-Morpork's first newspaper and the one the opposition has started while The Watch has to solve the almost-murder Veterinari is accused of. Maybe the truth can help?<br><br>Generally, I don't like industrial revolutions or stories about them. Yes, yes, I know: we owe an industrial revolution a lot - but that goes for the bad stuff as well! Similarly, I prefer Death, the witches or Vimes with a loaded dragon as a rifle. Like a cat, I don't like change much. Hence, you can imagine my hesitation.<br><br>Nevertheless, this is Terry Pratchett. His craftmanship is undeniable (I dare you to say differently) and the way he slips in acute observations and hits certain readers over the head with a baseball bat regarding profound topics such as gender identity / gender fluidity - and in a sub-clause, too - while we're seeing the printing press not only being invented but also challenged and used; while we're following a man determined to serve the truth; while we learn about all the ways the truth can be twisted and corrupted ... it's quite wondrous to behold. One moment you're simply happy to see Vimes in a small role, the next that same Vimes throws a seemingly innocent line at you about accountability (how police officers are accountable to people and rulers - something quantifiable - while journalists say they are accountable to something so obscure as "the truth") that hits you and hits you hard (because the line, of course, isn't innocent at all).<br><br>And then Otto comes around the corner, keeping his vampire fangs in check by singing the most funny songs and revolutionizing photography. Oh, and the „click - oomph“ of Otto's photography device has got to be one of the best sound effects in an audiobook EVER. :D<br><br>If anyone asked me to describe this series exactly, I couldn't. It's humorous, often darkly so, but it's also deep in a beautiful and yet modest way, and the characters are not just unique but like family. It's absurd and yet makes perfect sense. I honestly believe that it takes a special kind of author to pull this sort of thing off and I have yet to meet anyone who could even only hope to hold a candle to Sir Terry.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561572643i/27730279._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="8835651190838913166" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13437479430679008325" style="display:none"><i>The Truth</i> is the second book in the <i>Industrial Revolution</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. The first book had been <i>Moving Pictures</i> and was one of my least favorite, so I was very pleasantly surprised by this one. I enjoyed it quite a bit. <br /><br />In this book, newspapers are introduced to Ankh-Morpork. Meanwhile, there’s a plot against Lord Vetinari (yes, <i>another</i> one!) to frame him for a crime. The story was funny and interesting, with some deeper commentary sandwiched within the silliness. I really liked the main character, William de Worde. Since the story is set in Ankh-Morpork, we also see some other familiar characters from the various subseries. Most of the page time goes to William, though, or to the people involved in carrying out the Vetinari plot.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_20972069" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20972069" />
    <a title="Kristen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1116185-kristen"><img alt="Kristen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241138579p2/1116185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20972069?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristen" href="/user/show/1116185-kristen">Kristen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1116185-kristen?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20972069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14087124221520436230">Reviewing Pratchett is always hard, I absolutely adore most of his books, and his literary cannon is huge. I have been reading all of the Discworld books in chronological order and have finally arrived at “The Truth,” the twenty-fifth book in the series. “The Truth” introduces the character of William de Worde, a young son of a noble who chooses not to follow in his father’s footsteps, rather attempting to make his own way in the world. Late one night the local rumor that Dwarves have found a wa</span>
  <span id="freeText14087124221520436230" style="display:none">Reviewing Pratchett is always hard, I absolutely adore most of his books, and his literary cannon is huge. I have been reading all of the Discworld books in chronological order and have finally arrived at “The Truth,” the twenty-fifth book in the series. “The Truth” introduces the character of William de Worde, a young son of a noble who chooses not to follow in his father’s footsteps, rather attempting to make his own way in the world. Late one night the local rumor that Dwarves have found a way to turn lead into gold comes to light right in front of de Worde. Lead can be turned into gold if you use the lead to make a printing press, and manage to find an excellent writer like de Worde to start Ankh-Morpork’s first newspaper. <br /><br />As with all of Pratchett’s books, we have the evolution of the newspaper over a matter of a week rather than the hundred or so years that it took in our world. Soon there is competition across the street with headlines like “Woman gives birth to snakes” and “Man abducted by Demons.” William de Worde however, is obsessed with truth, stories come flooding in, and soon he has hired Sacharissa as a story writer, and Otto the vampire as a photographer. I have to admit that the first time Otto takes a picture for the paper literally cause me to snort my drink out through my nose, and tears to come to my eyes from laughing so hard. I think that scene is one of the funniest moments I have ever read in a book. Sacharissa is the daughter of an engraver who becomes quite the excellent reporter, and ends up being key to the discovery of the truth at the end of the book. Otto, a vampire from Uberwald has joined the temperance group and given up the red stuff, not that he doesn’t have his moments, but he tries so hard to keep himself under control.<br /><br />William’s struggle as the head of the newspaper suddenly is flung into high gear when the Patrician is accused of murder. At this point the book begins satirizing the Watergate scandal complete with the anonymous tipster who is never seen (though readers of other discworld books will figure out who the tipster is fairly quickly). The guards, specifically Vimes, figure heavily into this book as they go about trying to discern what actually occurred with the Patrician. This book has one of the stronger plots in a Discworld book, rather than the humor coming from the plot, the plot is rather serious and the characters involved are the source of the humor. I personally find this to be a much better book than some of his earlier works because it feels as though it has more control over itself and doesn’t sacrifice for a joke except in some minor areas involving the bad guys.<br /><br />Speaking of the bad guys, we have Mr. Pin and Mr. Tulip… the bad guys from the Looney Toons, you know the ones… the big dumb guy and the little wise a__. They have been hired by a group of “concerned citizens” to set up the Patrician… these concerned citizens are of course, from the upper crust of society with a very defined idea of who should actually be in charge (preferably someone very dumb who will do what they tell him). I personally did not enjoy the bits with Mr. Pin and Mr. Tulip (who has a cursing problem and an obsession with trying to get high, even though it’s never stated outright). I found them to be rather tedious though I know others would find them to be the most humorous part of the book. For those of you who either read these with your children or let your little ones read discworld books (we listen to many of them on audio CD while on road trips, our children think they are hysterical). I don’t know that I would hand this to a child under 12… possibly under 14. The main reason being that Mr. Tulip snorts anything he can get his hands on, though most of the time he’s completely unsuccessful, having snorted mothballs, flour, flea powder etc, I’m not sure that I’d want my kids hearing that (my kids are 7 and 10).<br /><br />As a whole this book is brilliantly funny and the satire is genius. This will be funnier to people who are familiar with the press, particularly writing for papers and those with a pretty good idea of how Watergate played out. Although I considered making this a four star book because of my dislike for Mr. Pin and Mr. Tulip… the fact that the scene with Otto made my drink exit my nose bumped it back up to a five star book. I highly recommend it to Discworld fans and those who are considering becoming Discworld fans.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14087124221520436230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23767020" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23767020" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1177534-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593697601p2/1177534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23767020?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1177534-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=obscure-references-book-group">obscure-references-book-group</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=read-in-2008">read-in-2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=to-be-read-challenge">to-be-read-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23767020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1166277302408769172">A lot of your enjoyment of Terry Pratchett's DiscWorld series comes down to your awareness of the object of Pratchett's satire. In the case of "The Truth," it's the world of newspapers and journalism in general. Having a background in this, I found a lot of Pratchett's zingers and satire to be dead-on accurate in their humor and observation.<br/><br/>What I didn't find quite as spot-on was some of the twists and turns of the novel. For one thing, the identity of who is behind the elaborate conspiracy is s</span>
  <span id="freeText1166277302408769172" style="display:none">A lot of your enjoyment of Terry Pratchett's DiscWorld series comes down to your awareness of the object of Pratchett's satire. In the case of "The Truth," it's the world of newspapers and journalism in general. Having a background in this, I found a lot of Pratchett's zingers and satire to be dead-on accurate in their humor and observation.<br /><br />What I didn't find quite as spot-on was some of the twists and turns of the novel. For one thing, the identity of who is behind the elaborate conspiracy is so easily deduced that it ruins some of the driving force of the last half of the novel. Of course, the problem is that the readers know the identity (or can deduce it easily if you're paying attention), while the characters don't because they don't have as much information as we do. It's a case of the reader being a bit too omniscient for his or her own good and ruining the final revelation a bit.<br /><br />Another issue is the speed at which things occur. William DeWorde goes from hand-carving a monthly newsletter for five at-home benefactors to running a newspaper complete with moveable type press within a week. Pratchett works too hard to pile on absurdity after absurdity as the newspaper takes off in ways that William can't expect and doesn't prepare for. Pratchett works too hard to make a few funny observances by compresing the timeline and making the story feel a bit rushed at points.<br /><br />Which a lot of this can be forgiven with Pratchett being his typical self and finding unique ways to put words together to be both thought-provoking and funny. Once again, Pratchett has this way of finding just the exact right turn of phrase and combination of words to make what he's doing appear completely effortless. But if you step back and look at it, you realize exactly what he's doing and how he's doing it. And that alone makes "The Truth" worth appreciating. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1166277302408769172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12146737439309150683">The press comes to Ankh-Morpork. Chaos, of course, ensues. <br/><br/>William de Worde, a ne'er do well noble who is ashamed of his noble heritage, has until now made his living by painstakingly collecting news and sending off a sort of newsletter to foreign peoples of import who are willing to pay him for it. Then some dwarfs roll into Ankh-Morpork with something called a printing press, and suddenly, what if they printed a bunch of them and sold them for like, fifty cents? And maybe hired some people to </span>
  <span id="freeText12146737439309150683" style="display:none">The press comes to Ankh-Morpork. Chaos, of course, ensues. <br /><br />William de Worde, a ne'er do well noble who is ashamed of his noble heritage, has until now made his living by painstakingly collecting news and sending off a sort of newsletter to foreign peoples of import who are willing to pay him for it. Then some dwarfs roll into Ankh-Morpork with something called a printing press, and suddenly, what if they printed a bunch of them and sold them for like, fifty cents? And maybe hired some people to sell them? And maybe pay some people to tell them things that are going on in town? Like bake sales and fires and robberies and whatnot. <br /><br />So before long William is the owner of a successful business that employs several dwarfs, a teetotaling vampire, a troll, and a handful of vagabonds. He also has a bunch of money, and a bunch of trouble on his hands.<br /><br />This is a great satire on the business of news. Lots of fun stuff about what makes news, what people will believe, the responsibility of people who run newspapers, etc. Plus it's funny as hell. Running through this is a little conspiracy to discredit and replace Lord Vetinari, and I got such a kick out of the bad guys. They're hard to explain though, so I'm not going to try. <br /><br />I was in the perfect mood for this. I wanted something silly with a little kick, and that's what I got here. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12146737439309150683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1325111510963828260">I read this about 15 years ago and quite enjoyed the satire and parody about "the truth" that's printed in newspapers (if it's published, it's ink-validated). It's about time for a re-read. Or maybe I'll buy the digital audiobook and listen to it, if the sound quality is good. This book comes after <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63720.The_Fifth_Elephant__Discworld___24__City_Watch__5_' title='The Fifth Elephant (Discworld, #24; City Watch #5) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Fifth Elephant</a> in sequence, but could be read as a stand-alone.<br/><br/>This book includes some of my favorite characters — "people" I first met in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_' title='Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Guards! Guards!</a> —Lord Vetinari, Samuel Vimes, and several</span>
  <span id="freeText1325111510963828260" style="display:none">I read this about 15 years ago and quite enjoyed the satire and parody about "the truth" that's printed in newspapers (if it's published, it's ink-validated). It's about time for a re-read. Or maybe I'll buy the digital audiobook and listen to it, if the sound quality is good. This book comes after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63720.The_Fifth_Elephant__Discworld___24__City_Watch__5_" title="The Fifth Elephant (Discworld, #24; City Watch #5) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Elephant</a> in sequence, but could be read as a stand-alone.<br /><br />This book includes some of my favorite characters — "people" I first met in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_" title="Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a> —Lord Vetinari, Samuel Vimes, and several other members of the City Watch, including Gaspode the talking dog. New characters (some return in future sequels) include the innocent newspaper editor William de Worde, the self-disciplined photographer Otto (a vampire-on-the-wagon) and the fairly bland Sacharissa Cripslock (caps lock), female reporter.<br /><br />Apparently, this book is not classified as part of the City Watch sub-series because the City Watch characters have limited roles and are seen mainly from de Worde's perspective. Personally, I do classify it as City Watch. Where else would it go?<br /><br /><b>PLOT (no spoilers):</b> The printing press with movable type comes suddenly into the flat world — on the heels of the invention of photographs and the film industry in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a>. With wild enthusiasm <b>
  <i>The Ankh-Morpork Times </i>
</b> is embraced by eager citizens as the newspaper transforms the worlds of society, politics, and thief-taking. Overnight, it mesmerizes and captivates Ankh-Morpork, with everyone wanting to see their name (and age) in print.<br /><br /> Soon we have a "serious" newspaper like The Times competing with a scandal rag, The Inquirer. Funny stuff. <br /><br />And William de Worde gets to know the City Watch, with the Times office's located near the coppers' favorite pub — The Bucket on Gleam Street, just across the Pon's Bridge from the Pseudopolis Yard Watch House. But more importantly, there's also a mystery. A who-dun-it that reaches all the way into the oblong office. That's why the City Watch and Gaspode the dog gets involved, helping William de Worde and Sacharissa investigate the charges of embezzlement and attempted murder against Havelock Vetinari.<br /><br /><b>Plot, Many Big Spoilers Ahoy!! </b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The New Firm comes to Ankh-Morpork, entering the city through the Water Gate (setting up multiple allusions to the Nixon Watergate Scandal, Deep Throat (Deep Bone / Wuffles the dog) and taped recordings (the Dis-Organizer files). The New Firm is comprised of Mr. Pin and Mr. Tulip, a duo of criminals hired by the 'Committee to Unelect the Patrician'. Pin and Tulip kidnap a Vetinari-doppelgänger named Charlie, planning to use him to frame Vetinari. However, their original plan goes awry and they are forced to improvise. Soon, Vetinari is arrested, an investigation opens, and new revelations are revealed through the talking dog Wuffles /Deep Bone (we first met Wuffle's translator, Gaspode, in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400354.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch__2_" title="Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a>). With the City Watch, Gaspode, and The Times following the leads, the New Firm and The Committee is found out out eventually (especially by the heroic efforts of William the editor and Otto the vampire with "dark light" iconography).  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The book ends with Captain Carrot making the front page for rescuing a little old lady from a runaway beer cart. <br /><br />Time for a re-read. Definitely.<br /><br />By the way, Pratchett worked in the news industry for some time, before he became a full-time novelist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1325111510963828260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10618914613924779434">An ultimately slight tale with a degree of laxness in the telling, but brought to life by passion and specificity - this is a novel about journalism, and clearly draws on Pratchett's experience in the field, both in its details and in its complex and unresolved ethical-political debates. The book also benefits from some of Pratchett's best villains, and is an enjoyable and frequently laugh-out-loud-funny read throughout.<br/><br/>Not one of my favourites, and the jaded reader may identify the flaws that w</span>
  <span id="freeText10618914613924779434" style="display:none">An ultimately slight tale with a degree of laxness in the telling, but brought to life by passion and specificity - this is a novel about journalism, and clearly draws on Pratchett's experience in the field, both in its details and in its complex and unresolved ethical-political debates. The book also benefits from some of Pratchett's best villains, and is an enjoyable and frequently laugh-out-loud-funny read throughout.<br /><br />Not one of my favourites, and the jaded reader may identify the flaws that would become greater as the series progressed, but taking it in it's own right it's a solidly good - even very good - novel. <br /><br />You can read my full review <a href="https://vacuouswastrel.wordpress.com/2015/05/03/the-truth-by-terry-pratchett/" rel="nofollow noopener">on my blog</a>. Or you can read my reviews of any of the preceding Discworld novels in <a href="https://vacuouswastrel.wordpress.com/2013/11/11/a-complete-discworld-re-read-project/" rel="nofollow noopener">my complete reread project.</a></span>

<div id="review_2604861456" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2604861456" />
    <a title="Ric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35905360-ric"><img alt="Ric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583635231p2/35905360.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2604861456?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ric" href="/user/show/35905360-ric">Ric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062307361" href="/book/show/18782560-the-truth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35905360-ric?shelf=2019-reading-challenge">2019-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35905360-ric?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2604861456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2970529534990122257">I really enjoy Discworld, but I wasn’t expecting to enjoy this one as much as I did. It was easily 4.5 stars, and I might even raise it to 5 stars upon reflection (I’m rounding up for that reason now). Which really doesn’t make much sense, because I loved the first Industrial Revolution story in Moving Pictures. There’s just something about some sort of modern day technology being discovered on the Disc that’s wildly entertaining, and most of that probably comes back to Pratchett and his brillia</span>
  <span id="freeText2970529534990122257" style="display:none">I really enjoy Discworld, but I wasn’t expecting to enjoy this one as much as I did. It was easily 4.5 stars, and I might even raise it to 5 stars upon reflection (I’m rounding up for that reason now). Which really doesn’t make much sense, because I loved the first Industrial Revolution story in Moving Pictures. There’s just something about some sort of modern day technology being discovered on the Disc that’s wildly entertaining, and most of that probably comes back to Pratchett and his brilliance. <br /><br />I enjoyed the main character William de Worde, even if he was very similar in nature to Moist von Lipwig. But he became Ankh-Morpork’s first journalist and newspaper editor basically by accident, and it was hysterical the whole way. His investigative reporting when it came to a crime that Vetinari may or may not have committed was absolutely great. And him annoying the Watch while simultaneously doing their job was fantastic. The side characters were pretty good too, especially Otto, but I always enjoy cameos from other characters around the Disc. We see Vimes, Gaspode, Dibbler (because would it be a Discworld book without him), and a very funny cameo from Death. <br /><br />Without giving too much away, I think one of the funniest parts of this book was at the end when William and Sacharissa were explaining to the new hires what was news. It basically boiled down to them saying that everything was news in the most roundabout way when they were listing the opposites of what the other was saying. It was very meta and very funny. As of right now, this is probably one of the 10 best books I’ve read this year, and it’ll probably finish pretty close to that point.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2970529534990122257" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5372012909532657491">April 3 2004<br/>Jan 1 2012<br/><br/>Having read it twice, I feel like I should remember it better.<br/><br/>***<br/><br/>December 16, 2014<br/><br/>The Industrial Revolution series-within-a-series are all devoted to bringing the Discworld out of medieval European fantasy and into the modern world. This is the development of the printing press and newspapers. It is a romp on the theme of great newspaper romantic comedies, with the clever aristocratic publisher solving a mystery, dealing with politics, and getting the girl despite a certain</span>
  <span id="freeText5372012909532657491" style="display:none">April 3 2004<br />Jan 1 2012<br /><br />Having read it twice, I feel like I should remember it better.<br /><br />***<br /><br />December 16, 2014<br /><br />The Industrial Revolution series-within-a-series are all devoted to bringing the Discworld out of medieval European fantasy and into the modern world. This is the development of the printing press and newspapers. It is a romp on the theme of great newspaper romantic comedies, with the clever aristocratic publisher solving a mystery, dealing with politics, and getting the girl despite a certain romantic obliviousness. Lots of jokes about people wanting things published in the paper (pictures of amusing vegetables), jokes about feeding the press, and rather more serious issues dealt with lightly: integration, new technology, old boys.<br /><br />Satisfying but somehow hard to distinguish in memory from the movies it riffs on.<br /><br />Personal copy.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1084503484" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5238423-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352775557p2/5238423.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1084503484?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan" href="/user/show/5238423-nathan">Nathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=rated-4-star">rated-4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=author-male">author-male</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=high-fantasy">high-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1084503484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13503701289615117466"> <a href='http://www.fantasyreviewbarn.com/2012/11/30/the-complete-discworld-re-read/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Complete Discworld Reread</a><br/><br/> <blockquote> “An’ then…then I’m gonna get medieval on his arse.”<br/><br/> There were more pressing problems but this one intrigued Mr. Pin.<br/><br/> “How, exactly?” he said. </blockquote><br/><br/> “I thought maybe a maypole,” said Mr. Tulip reflectively. “An’ then a display of country dancing, land tillage under the three-field system, several plagues, and if my –ing hand ain’t too tired the invention of the –ing horse collar”<br/><br/>You can always tell when my favorite author is on his ‘A’ Game and when he is off. When the plo</span>
  <span id="freeText13503701289615117466" style="display:none"> <a href="http://www.fantasyreviewbarn.com/2012/11/30/the-complete-discworld-re-read/" rel="nofollow noopener">Complete Discworld Reread</a><br /><br /> <blockquote> “An’ then…then I’m gonna get medieval on his arse.”<br /><br /> There were more pressing problems but this one intrigued Mr. Pin.<br /><br /> “How, exactly?” he said. </blockquote><br /><br /> “I thought maybe a maypole,” said Mr. Tulip reflectively. “An’ then a display of country dancing, land tillage under the three-field system, several plagues, and if my –ing hand ain’t too tired the invention of the –ing horse collar”<br /><br />You can always tell when my favorite author is on his ‘A’ Game and when he is off. When the plot for a book is a bit weaker than the norm the easy jokes start coming through. The obvious ones, more likely to come from the fun guy at a party or a start up standup comic. I think of all the bad jokes that permeated through Soul Music and Moving Pictures and I cringe. So it is with great pleasure that I will point out that nowhere in The Truth did a character shout out some paraphrasing of ‘you can’t handle the truth.’<br /><br />Finally breaking from his ongoing sub-series for the first time in quite a while The Truth is the first to feel like a success to me since Small Gods. While the last book in the series set the stage for the world to start changing The Truth finally picks and aspect of Anhk-Morpork’s society to change in the major way. And true to life what better way is there to shake everything up than by have the people learn what is going on around them; or at least the free presses’ version of events?<br /><br />One of Pratchett’s funnier openings starts it off, people speculating that the Dwarves have found a way to turn lead to gold. Just another example of Pratchett getting more out of a page and a half than any one should be able to. Quickly we meet the protagonist of the novel when he runs right into this gold making machine (or more accurately, it runs into him); a movable type press a dwarven couple has brought into town against the wizard’s long standing order against it. But money moves all, and as long as the Patrician sees no issue then it is time to proceed with this new venture.<br /><br />William de Worde has long told important people what is happening in the city and made enough to survive on by doing so (plus all the figs he can eat). Making copies was a time consuming process though, this new movable type makes it so easy. On a whim he tries selling these items to non-important people and quickly find the news waits for no one. Of course timing is everything and when the Patrician is suddenly accused of attempted murder de Worde finds himself working hand in hand with the watch to solve this case (without the watch wanting him around at all).<br /><br />As a look at the impact of free press the book is hit or miss. This little venture becomes a full force in incredible time; a must read after two or three issues. de Worde and his cohorts, quickly joined by a reporter by nature named Sacharissa, fall into the game so fast there is no real transition of learning what power they have quickly found (most of their struggles are against the norm and involve supplies and competition rather than acceptance of this new idea). And of course de Worde is only interested in the truth, in no way influenced by money or political situations; a picture of what we hope free press could be rather than any reality we live in. The cash driven yellow journalism is presented as the outlier, the deviation, rather than any sort of norm.<br /><br />But despite getting up and going so quickly the way they start interacting with the world around them is a highlight. A pen in the hand changes everything; the knowledge that things could be made public proves to be as effective as old threats. The City Watch finds itself in the position of being watched (whereas before when asked Who Watches the Watchman before Vimes was always able to ME). The public has to learn what role these papers actually play, and what role they play with the truth (sometimes in an over the top manner but this is a short book).<br /><br />I would suspect that this book is most memorable for most folk because of the pair of villains, The New Firm, Pin and Tulip. They are not nice people at all. In some ways they are nothing new; the obvious comparison is Croup and Vandamer from Gaiman’s Neverwhere. Old James Bond fans would recognize their style in the villainous pair form Diamonds are Forever. Hell they remind me of a diabolical Abbott and Costello as much as anything. Pin is the thinker, Tulip is the muscle. They play to each other’s strengths and finish each other’s sentences. But Tulip makes them something special. Maybe it is a gimmick, giving the supposedly dumb muscle a reverence for things of beauty (another nod to Gaiman’s characters?). But listening to Tulip wax poetically about various works of art, even choosing to use a balled fist to knock someone out so as to save an antique, is a complete gem.<br /><br />As an addition to this series The Truth is a welcome one, one of my favorites truth be told (feel the pun people). I am not sure it says what it wants to in the way it wants; it tackles little issues with an ease that its handling of journalism never grasps. But it is real damn funny, a kick to read, and basically a standalone outside of knowing a bit about the Watch in the background (something that was common early in the series but getting rarer by this point). Right now I am going to call it a top five Pratchett book, let’s see where I stand with that when I have reread them all.<br /><br />4 Stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="13503701289615117466" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17482580627902003266">This is now up there with one of my favorite Discworld books. I loved the humor and the characters. I found it funny that the further along William got with the newspaper the more demanding and shouty he got. <br/>If your familiar with Discworld this wont be anything new, its just another great Discworld book. If your not familiar with the Discworld, you really want to be.</span>

<div id="review_2054183910" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2054183910" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2054183910?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780552147682" href="/book/show/288680.The_Truth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2054183910" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2666097839665797204"><b>Ankh-Morpork Gets a Newspaper</b><br/>13 July 2017 - Perth<br/><br/>	Okay, I'll admit that this story was better that some of the last few Discworld novels that I have read, but I'm still not hugely keen on continuing the series beyond the next one, which I believe is called <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48002.Thief_of_Time__Discworld___26__Death___5_' title='Thief of Time (Discworld, #26; Death, #5) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Thief of Time</a>. While I'm sure there are people that absolutely adore everything that Pratchett has written, I'm one of those that sort of starts to get a little board with a series that seems to drag on for a little too long. Honestly, eve</span>
  <span id="freeText2666097839665797204" style="display:none"><b>Ankh-Morpork Gets a Newspaper</b><br />13 July 2017 - Perth<br /><br />	Okay, I'll admit that this story was better that some of the last few Discworld novels that I have read, but I'm still not hugely keen on continuing the series beyond the next one, which I believe is called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48002.Thief_of_Time__Discworld___26__Death___5_" title="Thief of Time (Discworld, #26; Death, #5) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Thief of Time</a>. While I'm sure there are people that absolutely adore everything that Pratchett has written, I'm one of those that sort of starts to get a little board with a series that seems to drag on for a little too long. Honestly, even television series have a used by date, as I'm sure many of us are aware, and I noted that despite it's popularity, even Star Trek quietly disappeared sometime during a forth series (I believe it was Star Trek: Enterprise). Which makes me wonder why nobody has attempted to turn the Discworld series into a long running television show – maybe the demand just isn't there.<br /><br />	Anyway, The Truth continues with the line of stories that has modern inventions making their way into the world of Ankh-Morpork. While this is technically only the second story in the Industrial Revolution series (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10__Industrial_Revolution___1_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10; Industrial Revolution, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a> was the first one, so I guess there is nothing technical about this then), I'm sure there have been other previous books that have had a Discworldian spin on industrial development (though the only other one that sticks in my mind is Moving Pictures, though there was that one about music with rocks in). While the title might give the theme away, it might be a little vague, so basically it is about how the printing press, and in turn the newspaper, comes to Discworld.<br /><br />	The printing press is, not surprisingly, met with some resistance. The wizards, for instance, consider that words have power and that things should be written down with precision. As such, simply having things being printed enmass will undermine the sacred nature of the words. Further, words can be very dangerous if not used properly, and indeed if they are intentionally misused, because the words can put things into people's mind, and actually control the way they think. The other thing is that the guild of engraver's want their piece of the pie because since the printing press involves engraving they believe that they are the only ones who should profit off of the venture.<br /><br />	However, unlike Moving Pictures, this invention is taking place in the background of a much larger conspiracy, and that is one to topple the current ruler of Ankh Morpork (Lord Ventinari). The thing is that certain people really don't like the way that he runs things, so they bring in two characters, which somehow remind me a lot of the characters in Reservoir Dogs (Mr Pin and Mr Tulip), whose job is to basically destroy Ventinari's credibility. In a way, as is constantly suggested throughout the book, the pen is indeed mightier than the sword, and while they might be able to kill him, destroying his character is just that much more powerful.<br /><br />	This is one of the ideas that seems to float through this story: the power of the written word. For instance, people somehow believe what they see in print, especially in a 'reputable source', such as a newspaper. However, the distinction between opinion and fact is becoming ever more blurred, and even reported facts can be coloured by the way the writer presents them. Mind you, there are certain extremist sites that pretty much attack any major publisher, including the Guardian, yet there are also newspapers, such as those own by the Murdoch Empire, which unashamedly attempt to influence the political atmosphere. In fact, during the recent British election, people would buy copies of The Sun in bulk simply to throw them into the Cor-byn.<br /><br />	However the internet has changed things, much in the way that the printing press changed the world. Okay, unlike Discworld, the newspaper took a couple of hundred years to develop, but that had more to do with the rise of the middle class than anything else – for mass media to work people needed to know how to read. Then again, people don't need to know how to read all that well, they just needed to know how to read enough to be influenced. Yet back in the early days of the newspaper, people didn't have much in the way of political influence, and the newspapers had little power over swaying the outcomes of elections (since their weren't any, and those that did happen were either rigged, or staged, or the franchise quite restricted). However, the idea was to hold governments to account. Yet, this has changed in that newspapers, and now the internet, is having a much greater sway over people's opinions. However, I'm not entirely sure if we are at that stage yet where the internet has a much greater control over the way we think than does the newspaper (though I suspect we are coming close).</span>
  <a data-text-id="2666097839665797204" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_89112856" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89112856" />
    <a title="Kurtbg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/174365-kurtbg"><img alt="Kurtbg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325621886p2/174365.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89112856?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kurtbg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kurtbg" href="/user/show/174365-kurtbg">Kurtbg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780380818198" href="/book/show/567670.The_Truth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/174365-kurtbg?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi">fantasy-sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/174365-kurtbg?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/174365-kurtbg?shelf=terry-pratchett">terry-pratchett</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89112856" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9634502723587966073">If you print it people will believe it. Terry Pratchett has fun with media by giving Ankh-Morpork it's first newspaper. If it's in the paper it must be true, otherwise 'they' wouldn't print it, would they? Just who are "they" and what drives them? The power of the press grows as the city Patrician (ruler) is accused of attacking and bludgeoning his assistant.<br/><br/>If you're still a reader of newspapers in the age of internet there's nothing more telling about the power of the press then the automotive</span>
  <span id="freeText9634502723587966073" style="display:none">If you print it people will believe it. Terry Pratchett has fun with media by giving Ankh-Morpork it's first newspaper. If it's in the paper it must be true, otherwise 'they' wouldn't print it, would they? Just who are "they" and what drives them? The power of the press grows as the city Patrician (ruler) is accused of attacking and bludgeoning his assistant.<br /><br />If you're still a reader of newspapers in the age of internet there's nothing more telling about the power of the press then the automotive section headline. The section is formatted just like the rest of the paper, but its always powerfully upbeat about the latest models of cars. In most magazines there'll be a header along the bottom and top of the page declaring 'paid advertisement' because it so obviously is. Not here.<br /><br />Another example is talk radio. Hosts usually declare to be the authority on everything and being the ultimate judge of what is right and what is so very, very wrong. They also do the most fantastically honest and friendly testimonial commercials. It's hard to sell yourself as an open and unbiased seeker of truth when you hawk products of whoever pays you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9634502723587966073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1259302499?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay">Lindsay</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10326608-lindsay?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10326608-lindsay?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1259302499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5738624252614040461">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads' rel='nofollow noopener'>SpecFic Buddy Reads</a> group in 2019.<br/><br/>The printing press has come to Ankh-Morpork and William de Worde is there from the beginning. He's been making a living writing a newsletter for a small group of nobles, but when he stumbles across the dwarfs who have brought movable type (and the wrath of the Guild of Scribes) to the city, they all quickly realize that the newsletter can go to many more people and be produced much more often. Thus the Ankh-Morpork Times is </span>
  <span id="freeText5738624252614040461" style="display:none">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads" rel="nofollow noopener">SpecFic Buddy Reads</a> group in 2019.<br /><br />The printing press has come to Ankh-Morpork and William de Worde is there from the beginning. He's been making a living writing a newsletter for a small group of nobles, but when he stumbles across the dwarfs who have brought movable type (and the wrath of the Guild of Scribes) to the city, they all quickly realize that the newsletter can go to many more people and be produced much more often. Thus the Ankh-Morpork Times is born, complete with editors, a photographer, articles about clubs and funny-shaped vegetables and de Word himself as an investigative reporter. Which is handy, because Lord Vetinari has been accused of murder and the Watch's hands are tied.<br /><br />I'm ambivalent on the Industrial Revolution series. I think they have their ups and downs. The ups are mostly associated with Moist von Lipwig and show the organic development of their particular innovation out of the actual Discworld and Moist's ingenuity. This one isn't quite that, with much of the Ankh-Morpork Times coming together magically like the newspaper industry in our world, and William's particular set of journalistic ethics just appearing out of nowhere. It's not quite <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10__Industrial_Revolution___1_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10; Industrial Revolution, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a>-level of magical insertion, and some of it does make sense, but it does so at a fast pace to get out of the way of the story and the thematic elements.<br /><br />Still, if you ignore that, it does make a lot of good points about the role of a free press in a society, even when that society is a fun-house mirror of our own.</span>

<div id="review_1871026953" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1871026953" />
    <a title="Blue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45442249-blue"><img alt="Blue" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871026953?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blue" href="/user/show/45442249-blue">Blue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871026953" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9291402165770986708">You know how Goodreads has the Spoilers box you can check? I clearly need one for Full Of Despair Over The Future, And That Is Clearly Impacting This Book "Review." Honestly I'm surprised Goodreads doesn't already have something for this. <br/><br/>For the first time I became depressed while reading one of the Discworld books. <br/><br/>I don't mean a misty eyed "Terry Pratchett is dead, and there will never be another new Discworld book to surprise and delight me" sort of sadness. I'm talking about soul crushing d</span>
  <span id="freeText9291402165770986708" style="display:none">You know how Goodreads has the Spoilers box you can check? I clearly need one for Full Of Despair Over The Future, And That Is Clearly Impacting This Book "Review." Honestly I'm surprised Goodreads doesn't already have something for this. <br /><br />For the first time I became depressed while reading one of the Discworld books. <br /><br />I don't mean a misty eyed "Terry Pratchett is dead, and there will never be another new Discworld book to surprise and delight me" sort of sadness. I'm talking about soul crushing depressed. <br /><br />Because the thing that causes me to reread Pratchett's books over and over are the well crafted and witty personifications of silly human behavior stretched to extremes, which are then batted around by Pratchett's wit like a catnipped soaked squeaky mouse by an excited kitten.<br /><br />But this time, despite the comforting presence of gargoyles, werewolves, dwarves, Foul Ron, and The Importance Of Always Having A Potato...the real world snuck in. I've read The Truth before and was delighted the whole way through, but that was when the world made sense. Today what was silliness has become disturbingly reflective of the America's current political system. Tyranny is coming, and not in the educated and well informed style of Vetinari, but in the greedy destructive hands of those who care nothing for this country, but only for their own pockets. <br /><br />Pratchett was an amazing writer. His works are one of the few (if not only) books that are original, witty, and well crafted enough for my oozing angst to find a logical structure to process unheard of in my lifetime political turmoil...and for the very same books to, when I'm in a different mood, be relaxing, funny and relaxing. Nuances of brilliance. <br /><br />Today, and for as many tomorrows as I need to, I'm going to try and hold out hope that the end of The Truth is predictive of the end of the next four years. That after some "unpleasantness" we can dust ourselves off, find a new balance, and toddle off to a more hopeful future. <br /><br />So all of this was really more of a review of my current pessimist, frightened, angry and despairing mood, and not so much of the book. The book is very good, and actually quite funny, and you should read it. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9291402165770986708" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
